<div id="review_3378855651" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3378855651" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41609617-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539156508p2/41609617.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3378855651?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/41609617-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780860680642" href="/book/show/78434.Surfacing">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3378855651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2872538526532299313">‘Surfacing’ (1972) is Atwood’s second published novel and as might be anticipated, does not measure up against the brilliantly conceived, constructed and realised classic Atwood novels that were yet to come (‘Blind Assassin, Handmaid’s Tale, Alias Grace, Cat’s Eye’ et al) and whilst it’s not in the same league as Atwood at her best – it still stands up as strong and accomplished novel and well worth a read.<br/><br/>‘Surfacing’ covers or touches on themes now familiar in subsequent Atwood novels – this is</span>
  <span id="freeText2872538526532299313" style="display:none">‘Surfacing’ (1972) is Atwood’s second published novel and as might be anticipated, does not measure up against the brilliantly conceived, constructed and realised classic Atwood novels that were yet to come (‘Blind Assassin, Handmaid’s Tale, Alias Grace, Cat’s Eye’ et al) and whilst it’s not in the same league as Atwood at her best – it still stands up as strong and accomplished novel and well worth a read.<br /><br />‘Surfacing’ covers or touches on themes now familiar in subsequent Atwood novels – this is all about a journey, a journey back home, back in time, into the wilderness, into a heart of darkness, confronting memories and past misdemeanours, of missing parents, failing relationships, difficult sexual politics, of a patriarchal society and fragile mental health.<br /><br />Whilst the final descent comes somewhat unexpectedly – initially perplexingly so, ‘Surfacing’ remains nevertheless a powerful novel, challenging and thought provoking by turns. Not to be missed – especially by anyone who appreciates the work of the utterly brilliant Margaret Atwood. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2872538526532299313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2798408385" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2798408385" />
    <a title="Nicky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/60653155-nicky"><img alt="Nicky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509416899p2/60653155.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2798408385?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicky" href="/user/show/60653155-nicky">Nicky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10098676850093466794"><i>“Madness is only an amplification of what you already are.”<br/><br/>“It was before I was born but I can remember it as clearly as if I saw it, and perhaps I did see it: I believe that an unborn baby has its eyes open and can look out through the walls of the mother’s stomach, like a frog in a jar.” </i><br/><br/>An unnamed Canadian woman returns to her isolated home searching for her father. She is drawn back into her past memories of childhood and those she has suppressed. Gradually letting herself go back to nature,</span>
  <span id="freeText10098676850093466794" style="display:none"><i>“Madness is only an amplification of what you already are.”<br /><br />“It was before I was born but I can remember it as clearly as if I saw it, and perhaps I did see it: I believe that an unborn baby has its eyes open and can look out through the walls of the mother’s stomach, like a frog in a jar.” </i><br /><br />An unnamed Canadian woman returns to her isolated home searching for her father. She is drawn back into her past memories of childhood and those she has suppressed. Gradually letting herself go back to nature, she re-emerges <i> surfacing</i> as the self she had lost.<br /><br />While I prefer Margaret Atwood’s other novels that I have read, by god can she write!</span>

<div id="review_3060737888" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3060737888" />
    <a title="Matthew Appleton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57003346-matthew-appleton"><img alt="Matthew Appleton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578773686p2/57003346.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3060737888?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Appleton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Appleton" href="/user/show/57003346-matthew-appleton">Matthew Appleton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780860680642" href="/book/show/78434.Surfacing">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=read-2020">read-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=1001-books-before-you-die">1001-books-before-you-die</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3060737888" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15073678966933967588">166th book of 2020.<br/><br/>3.5. This is my third Atwood book after <i>The Handmaid's Tale</i> (which I studied in college) and <i>The Blind Assassin</i> (which I read of my own accord at University). Atwood has always interested me as a writer but never particularly enchanted me. Here was the first time I was genuinely stunned by her control of language; the prose in <i>Surfacing</i> is wonderful, a true pleasure to read from start to finish.<br/><br/>The plot, rather stylistically, progresses gently, bobbing, or rippling. Things are</span>
  <span id="freeText15073678966933967588" style="display:none">166th book of 2020.<br><br>3.5. This is my third Atwood book after <i>The Handmaid's Tale</i> (which I studied in college) and <i>The Blind Assassin</i> (which I read of my own accord at University). Atwood has always interested me as a writer but never particularly enchanted me. Here was the first time I was genuinely stunned by her control of language; the prose in <i>Surfacing</i> is wonderful, a true pleasure to read from start to finish.<br><br>The plot, rather stylistically, progresses gently, bobbing, or rippling. Things are left hidden throughout the book, we can see them, just about, but they only surface towards the end. Somehow, Atwood has created the feel of swimming, or diving, with the prose. The novel itself is partly a mystery, and partly a reflection on the self: in this case, the self being the unnamed narrator. Her father went missing many years ago and she returns to the place of her past with her boyfriend and two other friends. <br><br><i>Surfacing</i> is a love-letter to Atwood's homeland of Canada; the descriptions, as I have suggested, are vivid and the musings of our narrator are engaging. <br><i>
  <blockquote>The blueberries were only beginning to ripen, the dots of them showing against the green like first rain pocking the lake.<br><br>The shoreline unrolls and folds together again as we go past; forty miles from here there's another village, in between there's nothing but a tangled maze, low hills curving out of the water, bays branching in, peninsulas which turn into islands, islands, necks of land leading to other lakes.<br><br>The forest thickens and I watch for blazes, still visible after fourteen years; the trees they're cut on have grown swollen edges around the wounds, scar tissue.</blockquote>
</i><br>Though, despite only being shy of 200 pages, all this pales slightly as the plot ripples on with its quiet intensity. By the end, I was almost entirely worn out and glad to read the final page. A sort of Atwoodian nature does take hold of the plot at around 75% of the way through, as we begin to realise our narrator is not of a wholly sane mind. <br><br><i>
  <blockquote>It was before I was born but I can remember it as clearly as if I saw it, and perhaps I did see it: I believe that an unborn baby has its eyes open and can look out through the walls of the mother's stomach, like a frog in a jar.<br><br>I sum him up, dividing him into categories: he's good in bed, better than the one before; he's moody but he's not much bother, we split the rent and he doesn't talk much, that's an advantage. When he suggested we should live together I didn't hesitate. It wasn't even a real decision, it was more like buying a goldfish or a plotted cactus plant, not because you want one ni advance but because you happen to be in the store and you see them lined up on the counter. I'm fond of him, I'd rather have him around than not; though it would be nice if he meant something more to me. The fact that he doesn't makes me sad: no one has since my husband. A divorce is like an amputation, you survive but there's less of you.<br><br>When you can't tell the difference between your own pleasure and your pain then you're an addict. I did that, I fed him unlimited supplies of nothing, he wasn't ready for it, it was too strong for him, he had to fill it up, like people isolated in a blank room who see patterns.</blockquote>
</i><br>But, <i>Surfacing</i> was only her second novel and it's no surprise that she has led a long literary career after such a book as this. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1604674641i/30343442._SX540_.jpg" width="500" height="320" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Atwood in Toronto, 1972: the year of <i>Surfacing's</i> publication. Photo from The Guardian.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15073678966933967588" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2498077425" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2498077425" />
    <a title="Nick Imrie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5906293-nick-imrie"><img alt="Nick Imrie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591436280p2/5906293.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2498077425?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick Imrie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick Imrie" href="/user/show/5906293-nick-imrie">Nick Imrie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/12071576-surfacing">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=literary">literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=place-canada">place-canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2498077425" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer182550312848468619">This is the sort of book that gives literary fiction a bad name: a story in which nothing much happens, just a collection of nasty, middle-class people being mean and spiteful, more or less subtly. One could argue that the endless ugly prose is a reflection of the protagonist's state of mind, but that doesn't make it any more bearable to read.<br/><br/>I suppose it's a good depiction of the self-hating slave morality of middle-class women just prior to 2nd wave feminism taking off – but really, you could </span>
  <span id="freeText182550312848468619" style="display:none">This is the sort of book that gives literary fiction a bad name: a story in which nothing much happens, just a collection of nasty, middle-class people being mean and spiteful, more or less subtly. One could argue that the endless ugly prose is a reflection of the protagonist's state of mind, but that doesn't make it any more bearable to read.<br /><br />I suppose it's a good depiction of the self-hating slave morality of middle-class women just prior to 2nd wave feminism taking off – but really, you could read Germaine Greer's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/98532.The_Female_Eunuch" title="The Female Eunuch by Germaine Greer" rel="nofollow noopener">The Female Eunuch</a>, which points out all the same faults but with wit, rage, and vivacity that leaves you hopeful and energised rather than depressed and loathing.<br /><br />There are glimmers of Atwood-to-come. Especially in the hilarious encounter between the different groups staying at the lake. One particularly unpleasant Canuck has been complaining endlessly about how much he hates Yanks, and spies a group of Americans out fishing, and rails against them, fantasising about attacking them (it's all blather of course, he's a coward). When the two groups finally meet, it transpires the other group are also Canucks, who also thought they were Yanks, because they all confused each other's baseball insignia for American flags. Despite all hating Americans, they all love American baseball. The silliness, hypocrisy, and projection of all this Yank-hating is very darkly comic, but it's just about the only hint of Atwood's wonderful bitter humour. The rest of the book is terribly po-faced and dull.</span>
  <a data-text-id="182550312848468619" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

It went up a star for me this time. I think this novel works a lot like a poem. It's about what you feel rather than what can be perfectly, clearly articulated.</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=newbury-library">newbury-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=reviewed-for-blog">reviewed-for-blog</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=mysteries">mysteries</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=unreliable-narrator">unreliable-narrator</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=novellas">novellas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2580477864" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13036053025561852109">A young Canadian woman has returned to her French-speaking hometown, ostensibly to search for her missing father but really to search for herself. She’s an illustrator at work on a collection of Quebec folk tales, and in her past are a husband and child that she’s left behind. With her at her father’s lakeside cabin are her boyfriend Joe, whom she’s not sure she loves, and their friends David and Anna, a married couple whose dynamic is rather disturbing – David is always making demeaning sexuali</span>
  <span id="freeText13036053025561852109" style="display:none">A young Canadian woman has returned to her French-speaking hometown, ostensibly to search for her missing father but really to search for herself. She’s an illustrator at work on a collection of Quebec folk tales, and in her past are a husband and child that she’s left behind. With her at her father’s lakeside cabin are her boyfriend Joe, whom she’s not sure she loves, and their friends David and Anna, a married couple whose dynamic is rather disturbing – David is always making demeaning sexualized jokes about Anna, who is afraid to ever let him see her without makeup on.<br /><br />This is a drifting, dreamy sort of book whose gorgeous nature writing (“Above the trees streaky mackerel clouds are spreading in over the sky, paint on a wet page; no wind at lake level, soft feel of the air before rain”) inures you to various threats. You’re never sure just how serious they’ll turn out to be. Will the men’s attitude to women spill over into outright assault? Will there be some big blowup with the resented Americans who are monopolizing the lake? (“We used to think they were harmless and funny and inept and faintly lovable, like President Eisenhower.”)<br /><br />Meanwhile, the question of her father’s whereabouts becomes less and less important as the book goes on. The narrator seems to have closed herself off to emotion, but all that’s repressed returns dramatically in the final 20 pages (“From any rational point of view I am absurd; but there are no longer any rational points of view.”).<br /><br />I suspect there may be a fair bit I missed; the book would probably benefit from future re-readings and even some exploration of secondary sources to think about all that’s going on. It’s maybe not as accessible and plot-heavy as much of Atwood’s later work, but I found it to be an intriguing and rewarding read. It’s from 1972 but still feels timely. And it was the perfect choice for both Novellas in November and Margaret Atwood Reading Month!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13036053025561852109" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_55240206" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/55240206" />
    <a title="Donna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/130454-donna"><img alt="Donna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1220824131p2/130454.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/55240206?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Donna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Donna" href="/user/show/130454-donna">Donna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/130454-donna?shelf=literary-novels">literary-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer55240206" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9526335263590007893">"To become like a little child again, a barbarian, a vandal; it was in us too, it was innate."<br/>--Margaret Atwood, <i>Surfacing</i><br/><br/><i>Surfacing</i> would be a very interesting book to <i>study</i>. From a literary standpoint, it's deep, rich, and powerful. If Margaret Atwood has not yet been considered for a Pulitzer or Nobel Prize in literature (she's won pretty much every other award), it's just a matter of time.<br/><br/>That said, I didn't <i>like</i> this book. The mood, images, and themes are dark and deeply disturbing. This is </span>
  <span id="freeText9526335263590007893" style="display:none">"To become like a little child again, a barbarian, a vandal; it was in us too, it was innate."<br />--Margaret Atwood, <i>Surfacing</i><br /><br /><i>Surfacing</i> would be a very interesting book to <i>study</i>. From a literary standpoint, it's deep, rich, and powerful. If Margaret Atwood has not yet been considered for a Pulitzer or Nobel Prize in literature (she's won pretty much every other award), it's just a matter of time.<br /><br />That said, I didn't <i>like</i> this book. The mood, images, and themes are dark and deeply disturbing. This is not a good bedtime book to lull you off to sleep; it's the stuff of nightmares. In terms of its insights into human nature, it belongs on a shelf with <i>Lord of the Flies</i>--although adults reduced to their most primitive state can be even more cruel, destructive, and frightening than children.<br /><br />We readers read different books for different purposes. Had I not missed my calling as a professor of literature, I might have spent months or years mining this book for its symbolism and literary elegance. As things stand, I'll probably follow it up with a good, light "cozy."<br /><br />Do I recommend this book? Highly! But don't depend on it to make you feel good.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9526335263590007893" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

This is not the novel The Handmaid\'s Tale.\n\n\nThe Handmaid\'s Tale (1985), by Canadian writer Margaret Atwood, revisits the Anglo-American utopian/dystopian tradition. Appealing to imaginative fiction and the novel of ideas, the construction of perfect - or nightmarish - worlds rouses the reader\'s socio-political awareness of the present and invites questions on the shape of the near furure. The Handmaid\'s Tale deconstructs the utopian narrative by breaking the chronological order of the female protagonist\'s experience into a time-shifting testimony, a quest for meaning and an exploration of self versus the other. The intricate play on word and symbol can be read against the historical background of seventeenth-century New England Puritanism, as well as the twentieth-century New Right and women\'s rights movements, while inviting reference to the postmodernist outlook.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3472.Margaret_Atwood">About Margaret Atwood</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/3472.Margaret_Atwood"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1282859073p3/3472.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/3472.Margaret_Atwood">Margaret Atwood
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13665333502051015573">Margaret Atwood was born in 1939 in Ottawa and grew up in northern Ontario, Quebec, and Toronto. She received her undergraduate degree from Victoria College at the University of Toronto and her master's degree from Radcliffe College.<br/><br/>Throughout her writing career, Margaret Atwood has received numerous awards and honourary degrees. She is the author of more than thirty-five volumes of poetry, childr</span>
  <span id="freeText13665333502051015573" style="display:none">Margaret Atwood was born in 1939 in Ottawa and grew up in northern Ontario, Quebec, and Toronto. She received her undergraduate degree from Victoria College at the University of Toronto and her master's degree from Radcliffe College.<br /><br />Throughout her writing career, Margaret Atwood has received numerous awards and honourary degrees. She is the author of more than thirty-five volumes of poetry, children’s literature, fiction, and non-fiction and is perhaps best known for her novels, which include The Edible Woman (1970), The Handmaid's Tale (1983), The Robber Bride (1994), Alias Grace (1996), and The Blind Assassin, which won the prestigious Booker Prize in 2000. Atwood's dystopic novel, Oryx and Crake, was published in 2003. The Tent (mini-fictions) and Moral Disorder (short stories) both appeared in 2006. Her most recent volume of poetry, The Door, was published in 2007. Her non-fiction book, Payback: Debt and the Shadow Side of Wealth ­ in the Massey series, appeared in 2008, and her most recent novel, The Year of the Flood, in the autumn of 2009. Ms. Atwood's work has been published in more than forty languages, including Farsi, Japanese, Turkish, Finnish, Korean, Icelandic and Estonian. In 2004 she co-invented the Long Pen TM.<br /><br />Margaret Atwood currently lives in Toronto with writer Graeme Gibson. <br /><br />Associations: Margaret Atwood was President of the Writers' Union of Canada from May 1981 to May 1982, and was President of International P.E.N., Canadian Centre (English Speaking) from 1984-1986. She and Graeme Gibson are the Joint Honourary Presidents of the Rare Bird Society within BirdLife International. Ms. Atwood is also a current Vice-President of PEN International.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13665333502051015573" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
